Mellors is by no means a good or perfect man, nor is Lady Chatterley the perfect heroine. However, there is something beautiful about their relationship which is based on sexual compatibility, rather than belonging to the same social class.<br /><br />Despite its reputation for a few well-written, but explicit scenes, Lady Chatterley's lover is a beautiful novel that asks many questions about the British class system and the notion of love being more important than duty or status.

Various contacts via on-line support chat, telephone support and visits to Barnes and Noble stores have yielded the following:  The people on the other end of the conversation basically have no knowlege of the problem and direct you through a series of needless exercises in hopes that something will work.  If you escalate, you will eventually get to B&N personnel who will acknowledge the problem and proceed to give you a non-answer.  If you compare this experience to the recent Kindle Fire problems you find Amazon.com stating they have a problem, what they are doing to fix it and an estimate of when things will be better.  Recent announcements tell you that Amazon.com has followed through on this and that the first batch of fixes are already available.  Barnes and Noble, acting in a manner that is the reverse of Amazon.com, continues to let those with the Wi-Fi problem live with that problem.  While you will not find any reference to the problem on the B&N site a Google search on \\"Barnes and Noble Simple Touch W-Fi problems\\" or a similar search, will give you a better feel for Simiple Touch owners who are struggling.  SOLUTIONS:  Drive to another location where Wi-Fi works (suggested by one B&N person). Your less technical friends and relatives or those who do not have a PC and rely on working wFi-Fi may not be so lucky.  Of course another solution would be to 1.Acknowledge the problem, 2.State and estimated date when a resolution may be available. 3.Fix the problem and announce the fix.  Amazon.com did this.  Why can't Barnes and Noble? Good luck to those without this problem.  You have a nice light, long battery life E-reader that works well in appropriate lighting. The Simple Touch \\"found\\" Wi-Fi at McDonalds but could not complete the connection using McDonald's \\"proxy\\" software where you had to comply before you could continue.  The Nook Color was presented the acceptance message from the proxy through the browser on the Nook Color.  I may not understand the correct procedure on the Simple Touch, but it does not appear to be obvious.  The Simple Touch did connect via Wi-Fi at B&N and at my local library. Technicians I talked to advised against purchasing another router as a solution as the problem may also exist for other routers as well.

Remember back in the day when parents were horrified by it and teachers warned of a break down in discipline and preachers were calling rock and roll the devil's music? He spent his childhood in Dartford, England as an only child. He joined a boys' choir and sang in Westminster Abbey for the queen. Then his voice began to change with puberty and that was all she wrote for that choir. They kicked him out. Keith was devastated. It was a betrayal. And so he turned into a delinquent, sort of, and to this day carries with him a deeply felt contempt for anyone or anything claiming authority. His grandfather Gus gave him a guitar.<br /><br />Keith discovered rock and roll while listening to the radio late at night in his room under the covers because he was supposed to be asleep. \\"Heartbreak Hotel.\\" Then he began to hunt down rock and roll and it's predecessor, American blues. Muddy Waters, Howlin' Wolf. He taught himself to play these songs. Then one day he happened into a man on a train platform with a couple of these records under his arm. Mick Jagger. The Stones played the club circuit where they were very successful. The band had accomplished what Keith and Mick had envisioned: they were a highly successful London rhythm and blues band. The two films have a similar central character: an aging former \\"Queen Bee\\", still beautiful, but also still stuck in adolescent, immature delusions about how her life should be (as well as a penchant for substance abuse). However, while \\"Bad Teacher\\" was a low-brow, lightweight comedy, \\"Young Adult\\" aims much higher, and although it has moments of humor, is a serious and often painful character study.<br /><br />Mavis left her small town home of Mercury to move to Minneapolis, where she has had a fairly successful career as a writer of young adult fiction. In her personal life, she has become isolated and lonely, filling up her time with excessive drinking and one-night-stands. When she receives an email notice that her former high school sweetheart, Buddy, had recently become a father, she develops a bizarre and unlikely plan to go back to her home town and win him back.<br /><br />And so it begins. Although affable Buddy is happy to meet Mavis for a friendly drink, he is clearly content with his life as a husband (to a fun and compassionate woman) and new father, and has no romantic interest in Mavis. Clueless, Mavis continues her pursuit of Buddy, believing that the two are meant to be together and that he secretly feels trapped in his small town life. The more Mavis continues her pursuit, the more she humiliates herself, revealing her immaturity and desperation.<br /><br />While staying in Mercury, Mavis does also make a genuine connection to Matt, a former classmate still physically crippled by a brutal attack in high school. The extras contained on the new restored Second Sight British DVD are absent from the Olive. Unfortunate. Reduction of 1 star. To sum up: 2 stars with a thumbs down for Olive Films. Given the mediocre quality of this DVD I may never buy another DVD from Olive Films again.<br /><br />There are, thankfully, alternatives to the mediocre Olive release of Rosi's Carmen. If you have an all region DVD, and every film and opera buff should, there is a two DVD Second Sight transfer of Carmen. You may get the PAL speedup with the Second Sight and you cannot remove the subtitles but at least you will have the two extras on the Second Sight. The other alternative is the Gaumont transfer that is available in France. It has English subtitles which are removable. So for those of you who do have an all region DVD player you have a choice between the bare bones Olive, the more fulsome Second Sight, and the superior French version. I really wish I had chosen the last.

0385523904|4.0|Apart from goose-stepping soldiers and images of military parades,  I knew almost nothing about North Korea before reading this book. I had picked it up before, but never got round to reading beyond the first chapter of a Kindle sample.<br /><br />Last week I saw surreal Stalinesque Youtube images of a featureless North Korean television announcer who had  a demeanor not unlike a female prison warden, officially telling the nation in a trembling awe struck voice, that their dear leader had passed away.  In the same clip were images of crowds of North Koreans, crying loudly in unison, as if their existence depended on producing copious amounts of compulsory tears.  It was surreal, macabre, fascinating.<br /><br />How, in the 21st century could a place like this still exist ?  How could people be so gullible, the deception so obvious, so complete? How had this come to be?  And why is it that in the 21 century, this place was somehow trapped in the somber decay of a discolored 60s communist time capsule?<br /><br />This book answers many questions about the machinery of this Orwellian superstate and how it manages to skillfuly deceive its people, how this deception impacts the lives of ordinary people and how the images which the world found so surreal, so out of place, would be completely normal if one had lived your entire life in the Democratic People's Republic of Korea. It gives a sense of the broad mechanisms that forces social cohesion and consensus, and provides a glimpse of how the system is perpetuated through isolation, misinformation, exaggerated half truths and outright lies.<br /><br />Though not a literary feat, the book is well written and provide fascinating insights into the lives of normal people. Interspersed with poignant facts, Demick manages to paint a heartbreaking picture of a dejected place, forgotten in time.  The text is immensely compelling as the reader is drawn deeper into an Orwellian 1984 to discover that North Korea seems more 1984 than1984 itself. The parallels are uncanny. As one the defectors remarked, one cannot but marvel at how the drab dysfunctional sociopolitical landscape of North Korea seems cloned from the imaginary dystopian world of Oceania. The similarities are truly astonishing.<br /><br />Despite being a mesmerizing read, the book is not perfect. The picture Demick paints is a little one dimensional, depicting a gloomy, humorless place filled with citizens who have been reduced to being obedient robots. Its hard to believe that people would so blindly follow a doctrine, without even a smattering of irony or splash of humor. Even the Russians, at the height of dealing with their failed communist system, would joke that they, the workers, pretended to work, and the state pretended to pay.  No doubt the lives of North Koreans must be dotted with at least some humorous stories of its contradictions and situational incompetence, though Demick fails to extract any of it, instead focusing mostly on sketches depicting the cheerless shades of a towering dystopian wasteland.<br /><br /> Though the picture is stark, I often got the impression that she tended to gloss over certain facts, or consciously chose to ignore them, to fit her own image of the place. At times I caught myself questioning some incidental information, and was sporadically left with the impression that some images had to be less uniform in reality than the ones she choose to depict in her book. For example, she sketches a place where very few cars exist, yet one of the defectors, an ordinary mother of no particular social standing is fetched, by private car, at her home, to be whisked to the border from where she would cross into China. How could an ordinary citizen in a place where everyone seems to be watching everyone else, do this without immediately raising a disproportionate amount of suspicion?<br /><br />At times her explanations for a particular sequence of events would not quite make sense. A woman's tofu business goes belly up and the explanation offered is the total absence of electricity for the stove that would boil the soybeans. Yet on the same page the mother goes to the countryside to collect bark and weeds and cook it for hours (presumably using firewood) in a desperate attempt to nourish her family. So why did she not just cook the tofu beans on a fire? Later on we learn that using electricity for cooking had  for years been forbidden. Perhaps, the reality was different. The real reason for the failure of the woman's tofu business may have had very little or nothing to do with the incompetence of the machinery of the state, instead other, more personal reasons must have prevailed.<br /><br />These kind of oddities occur from time to time, leaving some loose threads. The famine of the nineties clearly ended up changing North Korea, but how much or how these changes came about is not clearly explained.<br /><br />Despite these niggling discrepancies, this remains an immensely readable, factual and insightful account of what it must have been like to live in North Korea from the late 1960's to roughly 2005. The story telling is centered around real people and Demick skillfully weave each person's story into a web of historical events, creating an intriguing read that is very hard to put down.<br /><br />If you know little or nothing about North Korea,  Nothing to Envy would be an excellent starting point to understand the broad workings of this fascinating, yet terrifying place. Yet, if you already have a fair working knowledge of its squeaky, mostly broken state machinery, you may find her work a little bland.ce of electricity for the stove that would boil the soybeans. Yet on the same page the mother goes to the countryside to collect bark and weeds and cook it for hours (presumably using firewood) in a desperate attempt to nourish her family. So why did she not just cook the tofu beans on a fire? Later on we learn that using electricity for cooking had  for years been forbidden. Perhaps, the reality was different. The real reason for the failure of the woman's tofu business may have had very little or nothing to do with the incompetence of the machinery of the state, instead other, more personal reasons must have prevailed.   These kind of oddities occur from time to time, leaving some loose threads. The famine of the nineties clearly ended up changing North Korea, but how much or how these changes came about is not clearly explained.   Despite these niggling discrepancies, this remains an immensely readable, factual and insightful account of what it must have been like to live in North Korea from the late 1960's to roughly 2005. The story telling is centered around real people and Demick skillfully weave each person's story into a web of historical events, creating an intriguing read that is very hard to put down.   If you know little or nothing about North Korea,  Nothing to Envy would be an excellent starting point to understand the broad workings of this fascinating, yet terrifying place. Yet, if you already have a fair working knowledge of its squeaky, mostly broken state machinery, you may find her work a little bland.

0684135051|4.0|This is my first EZ book.  I am a young knitter, in my early 20s, and have enough experience to follow her thoughts fairly well.  If you get her book for instructions or patterns, I find that you will be sorely disappointed.  As this book was published in the 70's, a lot of the information is seemingly outdated and a few of her sweaters, old and not flattering in today's fashion world.  However, I liked this book for the insight it gave into the mind of a knitter 40 years ago.  The progress that has been made in the last 40 years is fascinating.  As a fashion major, it is important for me to look to the past to see the future.  This book, while it may be a bit outdated, is fascinating in the information she unwittingly gives to the knitters of today.    As a negative, I find that her train of thought while writing this book is a little like a long trip with multiple stops along the way.  She eventually gets to where she is going, but she makes a lot of side trips.  As most of my conversations go this way, I can follow along with relative ease, but I can see it getting annoying or frustrating for people who wish to get to the point of the paragraph about circular needles.    EZ's books are legendary within the knitting community, and honestly I'm not entirely sure that she is worth all the hype, but I found interest in the book none the less.  My advice for people who are disappointed with the book, try reading it in a different light, rather than as an instruction book, view it as a journal with helpful tips.  As soon as I did this, I found it to be insightful and interesting.

Peyton and Elena meet at Elena's Spiritual Counselor's home, initially by chance, then again at a weekly service, where they have a chance to really visit with one another. It is during this meeting that Peyton feels obligated to reveal her sexuality to Elena, more than anything one senses, because of past troubles she has experienced with straight women by withholding the issue. Moving on, and inevitably, complications do arise between Elena, her Children, her Husband the Pastor, and even a troubling member of her Husband's Church, that threaten Elena and Peyton's future and they both wonder, just what were they thinking when they got into this situation. Bottom line is how one realizes that complications experienced by these two Women were in many ways, no different than complications between straight couples, whenever one person finds a love outside of the marriage. B000003TA4|4.0|There's not much to say about this album that hasn't already been said, but I'm going to join in on the cliche with my first Amazon review.  Nirvana's second album, and first commercially sucessful album, 'Nevermind' was released in September 1991. Blah blah blah. It was the little Grunge album that could.  'Nevermind' is an album that you'll probably either love or hate. There isn't much of an in between. Personally, the first time I listened, I didn't get it. It took two or three listens until I really realized the magic of what has now become one of my favorite albums.  The album is no doubt very overrated. I have no doubt it would be much more appreciated if it were an underground album. But just because it's overrated, doesn't mean that it's a bad album.  Let's go track by track:  1. Smells Like Teen Spirit - The one Nirvana song that EVERYBODY'S heard, especially if they were tuned into the radio in the early '90s. And although it gets old after multiple listens, this angst anthem sounds just as real, raw, and angry as it did the first time I listened, and it drew me in.  2. In Bloom - I've really grown to like this song more as I've listened to it. And the words really go along with 'Nevermind' and it's sucess. It's really catchy, and it'll get stuck in your head. I promise.  3. Come as You Are - This is another one of the more popular songs on the album, and although I love it, it's one of my least favorite songs on the album. The main guitar riff to it is very catchy, and the song itself is great, but as with 'Teen Spirit', the song gets weary after repeated listenings.  4. Breed - One of the three heaviest songs on the album (the other two being 'Territorial Pissings' and 'Stay Away', which I'll get to later). The song doesn't say much that any other Nirvana song doesn't, but it's definitely one to head bang to.  5. Lithium - I go back and forth with this song. I was beginnning to grow weary of this song as well, but when I listened to it again recently, it's really one of my favorite songs on the album. It's really an anthem, and is extremely catchy and fun to sing along with.  6. Polly - A Nirvana song that actually has a story behind it (not that others don't, this one just stands out to me). It's about a story that Kurt had heard of where a woman was held hostage by a man, and used reverse psychology to get of of it. This is one of the slower songs on the album, but it's a really great song, and I dont't see myself getting tired of it any time soon.  7. Territorial Pissings - The heaviest song on the album, in my opinion. But I like it. Not my favorite, though. It starts off with Krist Novoselic singing in a falsetto voice, and then goes into the thrashy feeling that the song has. Another headbanging song, and also one I don't find myself listening to as much.  8. Drain You - Probably my favorite song on the album, for the exception of the instrumental break. I really like how it goes from the almost screamy vocals in 'Territorial' to the smoother vocals of this song. It's just a really catchy tune.  9. Lounge Act - Another one of my favorite songs on the album, but I don't have a whole lot to say about it. The bass part is awesome, and I love it when Kurt changes octaves halfway through the song.  10. Stay Away - Not much to say about this one that I didn't say for 'Breed' or 'Territorial'. Another thrashy, headbanging song, but for some reason I find myself listening to this song more often than the other two.  11. On a Plain - I've really grown to like this song more lately. It's really catchy, and has a really nice vibe to the song. I also want to note real quickly, what's with what happens right before the intro? There's some kind of squeaky-toy sounding thing, and then it goes right into the song. I don't know what led the band to do a lot of things they did.  12. Something in the Way - A really eerie song. It's also very quiet, and I have to turn up my volume to even hear the vocals. I find myself drawn into this song whenever I play it. It's a very nice ending to the album.   13. Endless, Nameless - This is just...no. I don't like this song. I've only really listened to it all the way through a time or two, but it just gives me a headache. Something in the Way is just a really soft ending, and then ten-or-so minutes later, this comes up and ruins it. I don't consider it part of the album. The title of the song pretty much shows you the feeling of the song. Endless.  So, in all...  Favorite Tracks: Lithium, Drain You, Lounge Act  Least Favorite Tracks: Breed and Territorial Pissings (Although I like them. I just had to pick a few). Endless, Nameless.  So, if you've never listened to the album, give it a go. If you have and you like it, listen again, and listen loud. If you didn't like the album or are closedminded against it, give it another try. It takes a few listens to really get it.  Five cool points if you read all of this.

B006IGZWB2|4.0|A decent movie faithful to the book. If you enjoyed the book, you will find the movie to your liking. On the other hand, \\"The Help\\" provokes reviews that have nothing to do with the movie. Or do they?  To my delight and satisfaction, I recently viewed, \\"The Help.\\" The movie adhered to the book without embellishment or editorializing. The casting was brilliant the visual capture of the times accurate and not the least distracting. I was especially enthralled by the fashions and automobiles which rendered me incapable of judging the story from a modern perspective; a neat trick.  Then I read the reviews on Amazon. To a person, they attacked or commented on the story, not the movie.  Do viewers or readers have a right to attack a story of the author's invention?* I don't know that I have the answer. I have visited writing clubs that focus only on the story and never on the writing. I leave those groups instantly. I feel the story is owned by the person who imagined it. It is their vision, not that of the reader or viewer.  Am I wrong? Is the random reader rather than the author to be the final arbiter of the story? In the case of the book, \\"The Help,\\" was it accurate? Well, it depends on your point of view. My articulate and literate friend Sonia is from the South and experienced that specific time and circumstance. She told me the story only skimmed the surface of the horrors and turmoil experienced by the blacks during the time depicted and that she enjoyed the book for that particular story.  The improbability of the author gathering the protagonists as represented in the book is very low yet did not distract her from enjoying Stockett's rendering of the events. Sonia also had the perspective of the whites who employed `the help.' My friend, Doris, on the other hand, told me the book was accurate and a fair representation of that time. She never lived in the South nor does she have black friends who were `the help.' To her, the book resonated with her particular view of that history. For Sonia, it did not. Which of the two gets to validate the book/story?  How is either opinion relevant to Ms. Stocketts telling of the tale she decided to relate? Are not her creative efforts and the artistic license she invoked not her choice? Her product? I am forced to wonder if \\"Uncle Tom's Cabin\\" or \\"Diary of Anne Frank\\" would ever have been written had the reader been allowed to determine which parts of the story are accurate or to be incorporated into the tale. Is it not the job of the author to portray realty as they see it and for the effect they hope to create? There are few white people who would give credence to Ralph Ellison's view of white America in the 60s. And those views did not, do not, erase the impact of the stories, nor should they.  I once wrote a short story about an 11 year old girl who experienced sexual abuse in her younger years and hence, related to adults in a mature-for- her-age manner. Her constant vigilance and adult-like commentary are entirely consistent with sexually abused children. My reviewers told me no eleven year old would see what she saw the way she saw it nor would they react that way. Is it my job to educate the ignorant reader that that is exactly the way a bright eleven year old would react and behave when sexual abuse is part of her world view? No. Absolutely not. And it is not Ms. Stockett's responsibility to defend her story. It is her story.  If the viewer or reader is in a position to control or edit what the author chooses to relate and who will read it, will there be any unique stories left to write? Is that not what an anonymous review hopes to do? Is the reader so naive that they believe \\"Schindler's list\\" is an accurate portrayal of events? Or is the reader to surmise the story is merely a representation of what the writer perceives and choses to convey? Can a 90=120 minute movie or 300 page book be expected to be an accurate history that covers all points of view? Or is that the opposite of what fiction and novel writing is all about?  Disagree? Many will. My position is simple. If you don't believe the story conforms to your view of reality, stop reading/watching. It is not the purpose of a book or movie to be an accurate portrayal of your reality. If you want a factual portrayal, seek out a documentary or history; which is no doubt tainted beyond your detection. But when you volunteer your commentary on a creative effort, is it not wise and appropriate to respond with your reactions to that unique portrayal and not the story?  Do we not assess Bogey and Hepburn traveling on a small boat in treacherous waters in terms of the art form rather than the probability of a spinster on her own hooking up with an erasable river rat? Can we not afford today's authors and movie makers the same respect?   *Not relevant to this discussion is who owns this story. That is played out in the courts as you read this. The point is not affected by the details of the law suit.

It also built suspense, kept me turning those pages at break-neck speed and culminated well with Julia's story.<br /><br />This story is beautifully written, flows well, and offers the reader a historical look at yet another horrific, and perhaps not so well-known event associated with the Holocaust. An absorbing and compelling read. Highly recommended.

B005FITIK0|5.0|We all know a woman like Mavis Gary. She's the kind of girl who everyone feared in high school, who was always effortlessly beautiful, and yet she had no personality whatsoever. She would do whatever was thought to be \\"cool\\". Her loved-but-feared status meant that she probably barely registered your existence at all. We assume that this kind of woman goes on to do great things, and becomes an amazing person who would have a whole new generation of people fearing her, but that is not always the case, and that is where we meet Mavis, in \\"Young Adult\\".     Diablo Cody will forever be known for writing 2007's hip-dialogue laden and instantly memorable \\"Juno\\", a somewhat controversial teen comedy, which served as a star vehicle for Ellen Page, ensuring that she will play rebellious teenagers well into her thirties. Diablo Cody has reunited with her \\"Juno\\" director, Jason Reitman, for \\"Young Adult\\". I would like to mention that aside from auteur moments in Cody's writing, this is nothing at all like \\"Juno\\", and that's a good thing.      \\"Young Adult\\" is the study of a borderline psychotic personality. It's the story of Mavis Gary, a woman pushing 40, who lives alone, with her obviously neglected Pomeranian, in her disheveled condo in Minneapolis. She is a ghost writer of young adult fiction, which is ironic, considering she hasn't grown up at all since high school. She is not emotionally matured in any way, and thinks that her living in a neighboring city of her suburban hometown means that she has a life. She gets an e-mail from an old high school boyfriend who invites her to a baby-naming ceremony, or something like that. Fully intending to get him back, she blatantly ignores the fact that he is a husband and new father. In her mind, there is something not right in the universe, and she intends to fix it.     We don't really know, as an audience, if we are supposed to feel empathy for Mavis, or if we should just feel pity. At several points in the film, we see Mavis laying face-down on a bed, either in her cold pigsty apartment, or her hotel room, where she appears to be dead. She's never dead, just dead drunk. Her character says to her parents at one point, \\"I think I might be an alcoholic\\", and the statement is largely ignored. Anyone who says this aloud knows damn well that they are, and the fact that her parents ignored this statement shows you what kind of family this woman came from. Mavis is definitely an alcoholic, seen repeatedly drinking heavily just to make it through the day. She may as well have been drunk in every scene. This is a study of a depressive character, but the thing that bothered me was that her parents ignored this statement, because they would obviously rather pretend that there is no issue. It's upsetting, but like I said, it shows where this woman became this way. That's good writing.      This film works because the lead character is extremely well written and observed, but also because Charlize Theron knocks this one out of the park. First of all, before I get to any other facts about this character, I must say that Theron plays a drunk very well. The last thing I remember seeing her in was her infamous performance as Aileen Wournos in 2003's \\"Monster\\", and it is going to sound trite, but she's playing a different kind of monster here. She is a woman whose main goal in this film is to break up a marriage. She gave so much of herself to this character, and she may as well be responsible for the film's success. I personally liked Mavis, a character who we're supposed to hate, or feel great pity for, and I give Theron most of the credit for that.      Patton Oswalt, of stand-up comedy fame, plays Mavis's ally in the whole thing, Matt, a guy who graduated with her, who she probably never talked to in high school, yet he is either fascinated by her antics, or just enjoys talking to her, because they become good friends. I know Oswalt from Cody's Showtime series \\"United States of Tara\\", where he also played the kind of character he plays here: the voice of reason to a borderline sociopath. He is a crucial ingredient to why this film is as good as it is, and had to be mentioned.     With \\"Juno\\", \\"Thank You For Smoking\\", and \\"Up in the Air\\", and now \\"Young Adult\\", Jason Reitman has a fantastic track record. I have enthusiastically enjoyed each of his films thus far. It's interesting for me to think of this film as a companion piece to 2009's \\"Up in the Air\\", two films about emotionally stunted individuals. [George] Clooney's character in \\"Up in the Air\\" didn't want to feel anything, and didn't want to get too close to anyone. The film ends with him not exactly changing, but moving forward in his life, ignoring factors that would have emotionally devastated the average person, and just moving forward. Meanwhile, Mavis is irrevocably stuck in the past. The best time in her life was high school, where she was popular and kind of feared, and now she is just pitied. Yet she is obsessed with making her glory days a reality again.      There is nothing warm, sentimental or happy about the ending of this film, let me just say that right off the top. The film itself is dour, bleak and emotionally brutal. The performances are powerful, and the writing is excellent, yet it doesn't have the warm, happy, everything-is-better-all-of-a-sudden ending that American audiences are used to, and that's a damn good thing. Most character studies about unlikable people typically have the antihero change for the better in the end, in an ending where everything is warm, and everyone is left happy, but Mavis doesn't get off the hook that easily. While the film, in a whole, is quite depressing, its brand of black-as-night comedy is refreshing and welcome. I would call it the meanest Hollywood film this year, and that's a very good thing.     Grade: A-

0553385461|4.0|This is my first attempt to wade through any material produced by Stephen Hawking. I am an amateur to this topic so I began reading this material with the expectation that some of this information would go over my head. I was surprised to find that he did a reasonable job in explaining the necessary knowledge and it wasn't until quantum physics was discussed that I began to have trouble. So based on the information itself and some of his nice illustrations I would give this book a very high rating.<br /><br />What held this book back for me was that as I read it, I noticed that he occasionally decided to \\"philosophize\\" about the implications of various subjects like a possible universe with no beginning(or \\"edge\\" as he might describe it), the limitations of a scientific theory, the nature of God, what it would mean if we could not find a unified \\"theory of everything\\", etc. During these brief moments, I couldn't help but to think: \\"Mr. Hawking really needs to consult a few good philosophers before he provides commentary like this.\\"<br /><br />Imagine my shock then, at the end of this book where just pages before he uses philosophy(although, perhaps unaware of it?) to speculate about the ramifications of various theories, that he gives philosophy a major kick to the groin on the last page of the last chapter when he says:<br /><br />\\"...the people whose business it is to ask why, the philosophers, have not been able to keep up with the advance of scientific theories. In the eighteenth century, philosophers considered the whole of human knowledge, including science, to be their field and discussed questions such as whether the universe had a beginning. However, in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, science became too technical and mathematical for the philosophers, or anyone else except a few specialists. Philosophers reduced the scope of their inquiries so much that Wittenstein, the most famous philosopher of the twentieth century, said, \\"The sole remaining task for philosophers is the analysis of language.\\" What a comedown from the great traditions of philosophy from Aristotle to Kant!\\" pg 142<br /><br />To me this simply seemed as though he felt that philosophy was an unnecessary discipline and could be set aside - even though even he was unable to set it aside in his analysis regarding the significance of pieces of scientific knowledge. He may not have intended to imply such a thing, but this is not the first time I have encountered this perspective among a theoretical physicist. The study of philosophy, logic, and critical thinking are essential to correct and explicable knowledge.<br /><br />This is still a worthwhile read (hence, 4 stars which is the \\"Amazon\\" equivalent to: \\"I like it\\"), but I felt it was necessary to point out an inconsistency on his part.for philosophers is the analysis of language.\\" What a comedown from the great traditions of philosophy from Aristotle to Kant!\\" pg 142<br /><br />To me this simply seemed as though he felt that philosophy was an unnecessary discipline and could be set aside - even though even he was unable to set it aside in his analysis regarding the significance of pieces of scientific knowledge. B0056BMK6K|5.0|What John Locke establishes in this book is that he accomplished what the aspiring writers who bought the book are seeking, and they don't have to reinvent the wheel to do the same thing. He doesn't brag about it, he just states facts. His financial success with numbers. Sales. Why charging 99 cents for a book makes sense, how the big publishing houses are now stuck trying to justify charging 10x as much for their authors' ebooks. It really has pissed some people off, which is why the negative reviews of Locke's phenomenal success story.<br /><br />Locke is a pioneer and deserves praise from those of us who aspire to do the same thing.<br /><br />Really, I don't have much tolerance for hoity toity writers who've been in the business for over 40+ years and who resent Locke's success. Get over it, people. It's a new age - a new dawn. Get with the program! Remember the big problem in the recording industry and NAPSTER? This is the same thing - only instead of musicians it's writers. I welcome the ebook phenomenon because the publishing industry has been completely locked up for many years, and a lot of great talent never stood a chance.<br /><br />Locke talks about how he, like a lot of writers out there, was not published, was not a 'name', and his past careers weren't literary. He spells out where he came from and how he got to where he is today, and explains the ebook phenomenon and why and how it opened the door for all writers. Those are all points well taken.<br /><br />He offers a detailed 28-point checklist. He gives you a roadmap for the destination so it doesn't seem like pie-in-the-sky pipe dreams. It's great to let a pin fall on a map, and declare it's where you want to go - but it's a different thing altogether taking the journey. The 'how do I get there?' problem is solved here. He lays it out for you. He gives you a checklist, then explains each point.<br /><br />This book showed me that it's possible for a no-name writer like me to achieve great things and become a best-selling author with some degree of financial success. We don't need no stinkin' permission to become writers...we ARE WRITERS, and the stigma that used to go with self-publishing is fading. He showed me that he did it, and challenges the aspiring authors reading to take him up on the challenge of following his system.<br /><br />He points out that most writers don't have a plan. They write the book and throw it on the wall like spaghetti to see if it sticks. There really should be more thought that goes into it, and he goes into what it is you need to figure out before you even start writing! GOING IN WITH A PLAN! Now there's a concept.<br /><br />The beauty of the system he presents is - it has nothing to do with publishers or paying a publicist. It's all Do-IT-Yourself, utilizing blogs, social media and more. ANYBODY can do this, but it requires work, and due diligence of course, and a certain amount of organization skills. And chutzpah. After a point, though, you could have a team of people helping out because you could afford to hire them!<br /><br />I wonder if the previous reviewer even read it. I pored through each page, and read some pages over and over again. His style of writing is fresh, entertaining, and he doesn't talk down to the reader, even though he's obviously very successful.<br /><br />The descriptions of some of the characters in his books were such a kick, that I actually bought a Donovan Creed Kindle ebook book, and loved it. I have a friend who is a Green Beret who is very similar to the Creed character in every way, so I enjoyed it immensely and plan on getting more.<br /><br />So not only do I consider Locke an expert worth reading on the topic of how to become a successful writer, I am also a new fan. Thanks to Locke for taking the time and making the effort to prepare such a wonderful resource for aspiring writers. It inspired me to get off my duff, and I will continue to refer to it as I continue my own writer's journey.

I also take good care of it, so I'm sure that's another reason why it's lasted so far. I know selecting a gaming mouse can be hard and especially obscure products such as this one that do not have a immense amount of reviews can also be a turn-off. Well let me just say that this mouse is everything I hoped for and more.<br /><br />The mouse I replaced was the Logitech MX518, highly regarded as one of the best gaming mice around, and it really is/was a great mouse. But alas I felt I needed to replace it, I just got tired of using the same mouse for so long and I'm glad I moved on. I gave the MX518 to my girlfriend and she is still enjoying it so it still has a happy life, but if you ever used the MX518 and thought it was comfortable, then the Inferno will be an easy transition because it's one of the most comfortable mice I've ever used. Honestly to see any review about this mouse being uncomfortable is baffling, they must have giant-sized hands or something. (What's funny is the MX518 is actually more money than this one, shows what popularity can do)<br /><br />This mouse has so many options it may be hard to understand at first. If you are great with macros then it shouldn't be too hard for you, but if you're someone who rarely has a use or just never made macros before, it can be daunting. I recommend looking up tutorials to learn how to make them. Don't blame the software because you're not too adept in macro-creations. I'm not too adept myself but know the basics enough to make macros if I need them for anything.<br /><br />Quick mention, it does only have red LEDs on the mouse but you can set it to different modes. Also, the mouse wire is awesome and I love the strength it has to it. This wire will not rip unless you intentionally want to rip it, I do not understand the complaints on the wire at all.<br /><br />Now if you're a gamer and see 4000 DPI, you might be inclined to go to a 5600 DPI mouse, all I can say is DON'T BASE YOUR DECISIONS ON DPI AMOUNT! I don't even use the highest DPI settings because I just have no need, it's as smooth as I need it to be and I game a LOT!<br /><br />Let me also mention real quick that a friend of mine had the Cyborg R.A.T. 9, a mouse that is three times the cost of this one and he had issues from the get-go with it, eventually having to send it back about a month ago because it crapped out on him. Do not base your mice decisions on price alone because you will be sorely disappointed.<br /><br />Some people also know about the Twin-Laser issue that other mice have had. It's a certain brand of laser technology that when you lift the mouse up and place it back on a surface, the cursor will jolt slightly. This does have that same issue but I have never noticed it affect my gameplay experience at all, and I play FPS, MMOs, MOBAs (League of Legends), RPGs and more and never once did I stop and say, \\"Wow, that laser technology issue really messed me up there!\\" In fact, before writing this review, I kinda forgot about it. The only time will you notice this issue is when you purposely do it and have a mouse cursor showing. It's extremely minor and will not affect you in any way.<br /><br />Last but not least I would like to say you should have this mouse on a completely flat surface. For the past eight months I have been using a wooden desk as the surface for it because normal mouse pads DO NOT WORK with it; when I say this, I mean the cursor will not be smooth and will be very messed up spinning everywhere, the laser is just too sensitive for surfaces such as that. I recently ordered the Razer Goliathus mousepad and assume it will work very nicely with this baby. If it does I will mention so.<br /><br />In conclusion and TL;DR for some people, get this mouse, it's amazing and the only gaming/casual mouse you'll ever need. The minor issues it has are nothing and will never affect your gaming/casual lifestyles.

There is no denying he's a natural-born storyteller.   That said. For as long as fans waited, I expected better.  To start with, I had every intention of re-reading The Name of the Wind beforehand, but ended up just jumping into The Wise Man's Fear when the mood struck. I spent the first several chapters regretting that decision. It had been so long since I read the first book that it was hard to rekindle the enthusiasm and I'd forgotten enough to be a little lost. The story's progress has slowed to a snail-like pace. If a third of this book was removed, it would've been better for it. Countless pages are spent on mundane and repetitious threads, while more exciting events are summarized in just a few sentences. When I finally got to the end, I was relieved that I hadn't spent any more time with this tale. (It took me more days to finish this book than anything I've ever read before.)  Rothfuss still proves to be very good at making key elements relatable to his audience. Much of Kvothe's story continues to center on the University. His academic endeavors, as well as his social experiences, resemble real college life. Kvothe is a very talented musician, and his music is another relatable aspect of the novel. If you're an artist, or even if you're just a no-talent aficionado like me, you can relate to the way Kvothe's music is a big part of who he is. As for this reader, I enjoy spending time in a nice, cozy bar, so naturally I enjoyed the Waystone Inn interludes the best.   However, I had difficulty accepting that Kvothe could be only sixteen or seventeen years old during these adventures. Admittedly, I'm a hard sell when it comes to child characters. Some authors have made me believe that a kid can fit an adult's worth of life experience into so few years -- Mark Lawrence's The Prince of Thorns, for example -- but only a very few, and this time, Rothfuss didn't quite pull it off.   It's because Rothfuss set the bar so high with The Name of the Wind that a three-star rating seems bad by comparison. To give credit where it's due, there are some moments of exceptionally gifted writing in The Wise Man's Fear. Nonetheless, when I look back at my reading experience, what I recall most is boredom.

I suppose the contract a reader makes with a writer is to go where he is taken, and it's a tad unfair to complain that you \\"don't like the tangential stories\\" (I, for one, did) or \\"don't believe the protagonist's character\\" -- I tend to agree, and yet, the characterization IS the writer's prerogative...<br /><br />All in all, there are passages of dizzying, phantasmagorical imagery contrasted by plodding, dull stretches and a few too many side roads to travel. So 3 stars seems fair. The call for more clarity and better editing seem warranted, too. Fans of Neil Gaiman are the most likely to be pleased, whereas newcomers to his work, such as myself, might wonder quite what all the fuss is about.<br /><br />Nevertheless, it's good to have a writer of fantasy/supernatural material reach and stretch for meaning and resonance. That he didn't quite there and wasn't too clear about the path is not a disgrace, just a mild disappointment.

I found myself recalling small details from chapters past as I read the book, admiring how a small seemingly innocuous word could have such a large meaning a few pages later. This fantasy novel doesn't progress through major battles, like other fantasy novels might, and at first I was a bit off-put by it. I was so accustomed to being fed scene after scene of battles that I almost didn't continue reading, a mistake I narrowly avoided. The magic is in the fine print. He's a storyteller, the \\"how they made it\\" is almost as exciting as the \\"why\\", and the consequences are even more so. The book strings you along by changing the character being narrated. At some points you're an honorable father, in others a fearful damsel, but each chapter leaves you feeling incomplete, you must read on to reach the cadence.<br /><br />All in all, this story is well thought out, well written, and pulls you in. 1401233066|4.0|This volume, FABLES Vol. 16: SUPER TEAM, collects issues #101-107 and is bookended by two self-contained stories. \\"The Ascent\\" kicks things off and offers a catch-up peek into doings in the Fables' lost business office. In this one-off, the blue winged monkey, Bufkin, hero of the realm of the lost business office, scales an ancient giant tree and somehow ends up back in his homeworld of Oz. Thereabouts he begins to foment a revolution, of which we can be sure to hear more in due time. Just not now. \\"Waking Beauty\\" closes out the volume and catches us up with - as Bill Willingham puts it - \\"the life and restful times of one of our long overlooked friends, as she continues sleeping on the job.\\" Just goes to show, nature abhors a vacuum. In the Homelands, in the ashes of the Old Empire, assorted warlords vie to become the new head cheese. One such has figured out that one way to power is to lift the enchantment from the sleeping Briar Rose. But he's not the only one to have worked this out.<br /><br />Getting to the meat of the matter: As things stand, Mr. Dark had sown waste to Fabletown and had driven the Fables out of Manhattan. Recently, Frau Totenkinder had failed to properly contain Mr. Dark, who escaped his confines, forcing the Fables to flee the Farm in upstate New York and seek refuge in the kingdom of Haven. There's nowhere else to go after this, should Haven fall. And here's Mr. Dark now, very close to ripping thru the mystic barrier that keeps him from stepping over into Haven. Day by day Flycatcher's magic is failing him.<br /><br />With Frau Totenkinder officially retired (fully deserving of her happy ending), Ozma has assumed leadership of the spooky witches what used to dwell on the 13th floor of Fabletown's Woodlands Hotel. Ozma means to assemble a strike force - composed of the fiercest and most fearless in the Fables community - to take on the malevolent Mr. Dark, except that Pinnochio suggests a loopy embellishment: Why not form a superhero team?<br /><br />Pinnochio, an avid comic book fan, persuades Ozma that - in the interest of \\"every little bit helps\\" - it may be worth harnessing the faith and belief generated by a population of Mundy comic book fans. And as you know, belief is what sustains the Fables, the Mundy's belief in fairy tales and folk stories and nursery rhymes and such. Pinnochio himself has fully committed to his cape & cowl persona, having parked his butt in a wheelchair, noting that a lot of super team chiefs seem to be handicapped so. I was halfway expecting him to cry, \\"To me, my Fables.\\" It's an indication of how desperate Ozma must've truly felt, that she'd go along with this whiff of silly.<br /><br />Willingham also introduces a side plot with truly disastrous potential. Bigby Wolf learns that his implacable elemental father, the North Wind, means to kill Ghost, Bigby's invisible zephyr cub and the North Wind's grandson. This all has to do with a proclamation the North Wind had issued ages ago, about not suffering freak monstrosities to live.<br /><br />After the debacle called The Great Fables Crossover, here's Bill Willingham again experimenting with meta-storytelling, except that, this time, he's doing it proper. It's a lot of fun watching Pinnochio do his damndest to ensure that the Fables conform to all manner of superhero tropes, working feverishly to get the costumes and the code names just right. I grinned big when I saw the superhero roll call which opens issue #104. There's something pretty priceless in seeing the likes of Bigby (a.k.a. \\"The Werewolf Man\\"), Ozma (\\"Super Witch\\"), and Thumbelina (\\"Tiny Titan\\") outfitted in garish crimefighting threads. And don't tell me that Brock Blueheart isn't playing the Green Lantern analog. The F-Men, indeed. F-Men assemble!! Heh.<br /><br />Since this is FABLES, the story arc doesn't take you where you anticipate. Bill Willingham has something else in mind. Frankly, how he goes about resolving the problem of Mr. Dark is more satisfyingto me, although I can see how other readers may disagree, seeing as how Willingham had seemingly set the stage so carefully for a dramatic and epic conflict, but then - bam! - here, eat some anticlimactic pie. But I like the unpredictability of this series, and I'm glad things didn't degenerate into a big stale superhero fighty fight, even if it does leave the \\"F-Men\\" sort of just milling around and Brock Blueheart entertaining some pretty lame aftermath conclusions regarding his deity Boy Blue. As an added treat, we finally get a glimpse of the person who had roused Rose Red from her horrid apathy at the Farm. Except that geting a glimpse doesn't translate to knowing just who in heck this person is. Willingham also introduces some food for thought, future plot points concerning a prophecy for Rose Red and the identity of the North Wind's successor.<br /><br />As ever, Mark Buckingham and Steve Leialoha's now iconic artwork is invaluable. These guys are the perfect artists for FABLES. They draw Bigby Wolf, my favorite character here, the only way he should be drawn. I'm glad he didn't get stuck in \\"superhero\\" mode for too long. Wolverine and Timber Wolf don't need that kind of competition.to me, although I can see how other readers may disagree, seeing as how Willingham had seemingly set the stage so carefully for a dramatic and epic conflict, but then - bam! - here, eat some anticlimactic pie. B004A8ZWVK|5.0|This movie and the book which originally inspired it undoubtedly takes some liberties with explaining race and gender relations.<br /><br />I watched it as a person with disabilities who did grow up with my own restrictions and laws. The book and this film does not deal with disability (except for fleeting references to cancer in Skeeter's mother), but I could certainly relate to the experiences and issues raised in 'The Help'.<br /><br />I give the screenplay kudos for publically explaining that the women did face discrimination and segregation on a regular basis. It realistically portrays the numerous rules and customs which governed the mobility of Abileen and the other domestic workers in 1960's Jackson. Limited mobility translated into difficult and limited life choices.<br /><br />When Skeeter 'really' interviews Aibileen, both are obviously cautious when she goes to the house in the 'Negro' part of town. Having researched State laws then on the books, Skeeter visibly starts to understand how much trouble these interviews could actually cause. It is different than asking a domestic to 'help' answer a newspaper advice column.<br /><br />Because she is not undertaking this assignment lightly, she then wins over the trust of Minny and others who were initially more skeptical of motives for wanting to write about 'their' lives.  They come to trust Skeeter, forming a multi-cultural sisterhood out of the business arrangment.<br /><br />Of course, you would be skeptical too after a life-time of mistreatment, having to clean up for others for inadequate pay. The domestics could not even use the in-house toilets of their employers! And they witnessed the abuse which these 'good' women subject their own families to, refusing to change a child's soiled diaper until their morning arrival.<br /><br />While I'm uneasy how much gennuine 'care' a domestic actually felt towards the children of their employers, arriving at a house and seeing a young child in soiled clothing had to be troubling and frustrating because of inevitably produced health problems. Proving the insanity of racism, the domestics were actually being stereotyped as the 'unclean' ones!<br /><br />Bryce Dallas Howard gives a great performance as the evil Hilly Holbrook. This president of the Jackson Junior League is so interested in collecting canned food for 'poor starving children in Africa'. Holbrook thne ironically chooses to ignore the extreme poverty and violent conditions her own domestic and others routinely face every single day because of Jim Crow Laws. Becoming a domestic was the only kind of work which these women were able to obtain!<br /><br />And then, several neighbors believe that only a 'negress' wrote the book-within-a-movie which eventually gets published from these interviews. The idea that another white person could be interested enough to listen to 'those people' is genuinely unfathomable: Domestics are expected to listen to families as part of their job, but their employers aren't ever expected to recognize their hard work.<br /><br />An exception is Ceila Foote who is herself 'different' in this era. She is recovering from a series of miscarriages and really does need an extra pair of hands as part of her road to recovery. She proactively eats in the kitchen with Minny and provides Minny with the care to escape domestic violence which Hilly and other employers never would. This actually shocks the usually-outspoken Minny. She recognizes that important cultural and social barriers were being broken--wanting them finally broken and actually getting to this point were two very different actions.<br /><br />As much as she needs a job, Minny is intially unsure if Ceila is playing some kind of 'game' with her. Unlike Hilly, the lower-income Celia is less concious of the racial barriers of the era. She and her husband (who actually might have harassed Minny in another screenplay) are sincerely nice to Minny.<br /><br />An obvious strength of this movieand why I was interested in seeing it was that the civil rights movement is used as backdrop. It shows everyday people actually working to change their difficult conditions during the era, something which should still resonate in our era of Occupy Wall Street. 0393338827|3.0|If you're looking for a fairly detailed description of Wall Street's role in the subprime mortgage mess, then this is a good read.  This is more than a 101 course on the cause of the \\"Great Recession\\" but it's not quite on a graduate level.  It's important to emphasize that this book is solely focused on the investment banks that securitized subprime debt and the investors that bought/sold these securities or their derivatives.  As a society, we like to have a single common enemy that can be blamed for catastrophes or an unfortunate turn of events.  Lewis understands this and panders to his readers (and potential customers of his book).  Investment banks certainly fed the beast of the subprime machine, however there are points further down this supply chain where individuals were intentionally committing fraudulent acts that were at worst criminal and at best morally despicable. Our author makes brief mention of the front line conmen that sold subprime/floating rate/balloon payment loans to customers who would have easily qualified for high quality 30 year interest rate locked loans for their home.    The collection of prophetic protagonists (Burry, Lippman, Eisman, Cornwall Capital,etc) was mildly interesting.  Lewis overestimated his diving depth into the life of hedge fund manager, (Mike Burry) who clearly made a good bet ahead of the MBS/CDO/CDS collapse, but the details surrounding his journey upon becoming investor missed the mark as being relevant to his investment.  Likewise, I found the \\"garage incarnation\\" of Cornwall Capital to be interesting, leaving me impressed with their entrepreneurship and strategic fortitude for their investment selection. The made a sound progression of trades from their investment of buying risk on the triple BBB rated mezzanine tranches of collateralized debt obligations to their savvy exit of these contracts to avoid the counterparty risk with Bear Stearns.  But their history leading up to their actions isn't all that relevantto collapse of the economy, other than fitting perfectly into the cookie cutter mold of David standing up to Goliath.  In a way, Lewis used this economic calamity as a biographical vessel for some of our \\"heroes\\" who's story probably wouldn't sell stand alone hardbacks on their own.   They say a rising tide lifts all boats, during this chain reaction of events the receding tide of mortgage defaults beached most boats in the global economy. (It's important to note that this was not limited to the United States) Lewis tells a good story, and as any good story teller would admit, there is embellishment and exclusion of facts that paint the compete picture.  All in all, this is a well written narrative that boils down one of the most important events in modern American history into 300 pages.

B000056HE9|5.0|1947 was Hollywood's second-greatest year, eclipsed only by 1939. With it's postwar optimism, it gave us GENTLEMAN'S AGREEMENT and MIRACLE ON 34TH STREET and THE BEST YEARS OF OUR LIVES, among many others. This 1947 film is a holiday classic. Along with IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE and A CHRISTMAS CAROL, THE BISHOP'S WIFE is required viewing for the adults at Yuletide. Of the three, THE BISHOP'S WIFE skates (almost literally) closest to the edge of year-round viewability.<br /><br />David Niven is Henry Brougham, the youthful, newly-installed Episcopal Bishop of a large Northeastern city. Loretta Young is his lovely wife Julia. Their marriage is heading down the road that should not be taken. It has nothing to do with love. There's plenty of that, and even passion. But Henry has become consumed by personal doubts. He owes his Bishropic to the machinations of Mrs. George Hamilton an unpleasant, demanding, but extremely wealthy widow who has promised Henry a new cathedral. She has also attached a list of vain \\"ifs\\" to the promise. Henry, who used to pastor at run-down St. Timothy's, is willing to do almost anything to see the new cathedral rise. Knowing that he is in thrall both to Mrs. Hamilton and to his own ego, Henry has become withdrawn, distracted and unhappy, and worst of all, distant to his wife and child.  He prays for divine intercession.<br /><br />Enter Dudley (Cary Grant), a suave, sophisticated, likeable and witty fellow who has a roguish sense of humor. He takes a job as Henry's assistant, but immediately informs the Bishop that he is an angel sent down in answer to his prayers. Henry is very skeptical and asks for proofs.<br /><br />While Henry continues to fret and worry, Dudley begins taking Julia out on the town for walks in the park, to expensive lunches, to stylish couture shops, to visit old friends, and, in a charming scene, to an ice-skating rink. Although Dudley's motives are at first angelic, he soon begins to fall in love with the Bishop's wife.<br /><br />While this forbidden affair of the heart is going on, Henry keeps finding himself in embarrassing situations---locked inside a room, stuck to a newly-varnished chair---and he begins to get angry at Dudley, whom, he rightly suspects, has designs on Julia and is not interested in the new cathedral. His suspicions are confirmed when a friendly cabbie asks Henry if he is the Minister who is going to marry Dudley and Julia, and then, almost in the same scene, a much-changed Mrs. George Hamilton decides to give her millions to the poor.<br /><br />Unlike other great Hollywood Christmas movies, THE BISHOP'S WIFE is a romance that has a very large dose of puckish humor: When crabby Mrs. George Hamilton wants a stained glass window of St. George Slaying The Dragon to \\"suggest\\" her late husband's face, Henry asks \\"And whose face should the dragon suggest?\\" When an old friend of Julia's serves some rather poor sherry from a near-empty bottle, Dudley turns the sherry into top-shelf Shiraz and makes sure that the bottle will never run dry.<br /><br />But like all great Hollywood Christmas movies, THE BISHOP'S WIFE has a simple, direct lesson about motives and motivations and the meaning of life: \\"Let us ask ourselves what He would wish for most. And then, let each put in his share, lovingkindness, warm hearts, and a stretched-out hand of tolerance. All the shinning gifts that make peace on earth.\\"<br /><br />It's a lesson that is as powerful today as it was in 1947. B006B3975Y|2.0|A funny thing happened about a month or so ago: The Roots and The Black Keys came out with albums around the same time. I liked the Black Keys' album, but hated the Roots'. I was all set to write a scathing review of Undun in which I lamented the downfall of The Roots, noting the unfortunate (for the listener) turn their career has taken, the softness of their music, the lackluster voice and bland flow of Blackthought, the vapidity of their \\"concept,\\" etc. All this changed, briefly, however, upon reading some of the many negative reviews of the Black Keys' album. Much of the criticism of that album came from long time fans who felt that their favorite group had changed too much from their initial philosophy and sound, had allowed too many pop influences into their music, and had basically made a crap album as compared with their old stuff. This sentiment bewildered me because I liked the album, and being new to listening to their music, I felt that these fans had lost perspective on music and life. I respected the opinion of the long-time fan, but really they all sounded whiny and crotchety, letting their expectations and nostalgia cloud their ears from hearing something that seemed to undeniably sound good. Then, looking at the overwhelming positive reviews of the Roots' album, I was struck by how many people, giving Undun 5 stars, claimed never to have heard a Roots album before, or claimed to never have heard of The Roots before they were on that late night show. It struck me that in the case of The Black Keys, I was like the fawning, new Roots fan, who, to me, sounded ignorant. But, in the case of the Roots' album, I was like the long-time Black Keys fan who sounded ridiculous and lacking in perspective. So, I decided to wait before writing this review.<br /><br />First of all, it is unfathomable to me that there are so many people out there who had never heard The Roots before this album, or before Fallon. It's not like The Roots are new, or relatively new, or have only had regional appeal. The first concert I ever went to was when I was 15, in 1994, to see The Roots while they toured for Do You Want More. They've been around for almost 20 years. And, I went to the concert not with my inner-city friends, but with some guys I knew from Ardsley New York (a wealthy suburb). From the beginning, it seemed that The Roots had widespread appeal. By the time I was in college in the late 90's, The Roots were well known to people of all ilk, being played in cd changers throughout campus. I guess what I'm saying is that every single person I've ever met in my entire life (in many countries) knows who The Roots are. They've never been some obscure group. Maybe not everyone bought their albums, but they weren't strictly some underground act. So, to read so many reviews of people who had never heard of them before this album or before Fallon to me is mind-blowing.<br /><br />As far as the music is concerned, all of the things I said in the first paragraph still seem true. I can't totally pretend that I don't know a lot about rap music, but I tried to listen to this album as one might who has never heard The Roots before, or never listens to rap. In doing so, it seemed that, fine, some of the songs had a pleasing-enough sound. I guess if this is the only album by a hip-hop group (more or less) that you have in your collection, then fine, I guess. But, if that is the case, it seems strange to be reviewing the album as though you have any authority on the matter. Not only that, but it seems as if this album, Undun, was made just for you. While some of the album does not absolutely make me vomit, it does when compared to other music that is similar. As much as I tried to keep a completely opened mind while listening to this, I realized that not only was it impossible, but it was unnecessary: people who got this album should know the context. If you don't know anything about rap music than this may come as a shocker to you, but: the \\"concept\\" of Undun is not only not original, but it is the basic premise of 99% of rap music. What is the concept? A kid struggles with falling victim to the streetlife? This is the subject matter of the vast, vast majority of rap music. This isn't a new concept at all. The concept of the album seems makes the content seem distant and slightly sentimental.<br /><br />The beauty of rap music is that it is rugged and direct. Not wistful and theatrical. This album sounds like something that was made not so much to be bumped from car radios, or to dance to at a party, or to sit around and think about, but like something that might be turned into a Broadway play. This is Broadway play rap. If you listen to The Roots albums of the late 90's, you will hear music of the same exact concept, but much more immediate, hard-hitting, and rhythmically complex. I am not begrudging The Roots room for personal or professional growth. I'm not mad at them for not being on the streets, or trying to make money, or whatever. But, to pass off an album as a \\"concept\\" album and have the concept be so trite; to be a bridge to rap music for many newcomers to the genre and have the music be so soft, smooth-jazz-Broadway-play rap, it's just disingenuous. I know that I am a crotchety, nostalgic, old fan, but you have to realize that at one time, The Roots revolutionized what rap music could be. The fact that they made rap music with a live band was mind-blowing. Blackthought was the best emcee ever. He was your favorite rapper's favorite rapper. I am a long time fan and as ridiculous as it sounds, The Roots' music has represented a certain aspect of who I feel myself to be. Over the course of the last few albums, culminating with this cynical effort (as it seems to me as a long-time Roots and rap fan), their music is unrecognizable to me. I won't be interested in their next album.ly not original, but it is the basic premise of 99% of rap music. What is the concept? A kid struggles with falling victim to the streetlife? This is the subject matter of the vast, vast majority of rap music. This isn't a new concept at all. The concept of the album seems makes the content seem distant and slightly sentimental. B004A8ZWY2|5.0|I thoroughly enjoyed this film. After seeing it at the theater today I came onto Amazon, as I often do, to add it to my wishlist. I then read the unexcited reviews currently found on the page and thought I should write a review to posit a counter opinion. The other reviews currently posted don't seem to have enjoyed the film, two major criticisms being that they found it melodramatic and heavy handed, comments like these make me wonder if audiences have become so cynical that they view any attempt at displaying open/honest emotion to be an attempt at manipulation. Everyone has differing opinions but I feel this film will appeal to anyone who enjoys stylistically well made films and/or anyone who enjoys stories about bonds/will being tested. In a general sense Spielberg uses the story of this horse to explore the human condition and explore some very well done set pieces/environments.  As I saw it, the horse is a metaphor for hope and how it can be shared and spread, how it can inspire and endure. I don't mean this in an oversentimental way but just in a very real way. We are all hopeful for different things, big and small, and this film is about how under the right conditions and with the right persistence that hope can avoid being snuffed out or lost.   The film, based on a play I have not seen and cannot compare it to, is episodic as the horse goes from owner to owner during the years of World War 1. The fact that the film was episodic didn't make it feel chopped up or give it the feel of a broken narrative. I felt the throughline of the film was the human condition and the travelling horse facilitated the telling of several viewpoints while exploring the excellent recreation of a time period. The acting was top notch by everyone seen on screen. To counter another criticism, the main human character, a boy named Albert, does show strong affection for the horse, a character in the film quips \\"come on now boy, it's not as if he were a dog\\". I didn't find this relationship to be strange, the horse simply becomes very important to Albert because as we see he doesn't have much to his life and the horse is something he gets that then works to make his life better. Perhaps I also never found it strange as I always found the horse to be representative of hope rather than as just some random animal.   The writing in terms of dialogue flowed and felt incredibly natural, as it does in most Spielberg films. John Williams score, while clearly a John Williams score, is the perfect mix of innocence, action/adventure and drama as the story calls for it. I would strongly compare the pacing and acting to Catch Me if You Can, as that film also had several major settings that shifted as the film progressed. I found War Horse to actually be better paced as it has a few more settings (about 5, possibly 6 in total?) and each one is shorter than the ones in the aforementioned film. Best of all the film actually ends when it ends, the story concludes and there are no tacked on or forced endings for the sake of pandering or overexplaining as many recent films have had.  As far as the directing, I don't know if one can compare anything to the seeming effortless magic he created in his early films or certain efforts from the 90's but this would be Spielberg above his recent best and closer to those older films in quality. Everything unfolds visually in such an easy to follow an familiar way while still being unique and involving. There are some masterful shots that recall the opening of Saving Private Ryan but in an artistically different way. This is unquestionably due in part to the fact that this film has been made family friendly, while that could serve as a criticism I didn't find the film to be aimed specifically at families, though it has been made to fascilitate viewing by younger children. Rather than illustrating the pure violence of battle as he did in SPR, Spielberg spends his time in this film showing the brutality of war in a creative fashion that suggests the horror more than displaying it. There is one such scene in particular where mounted soldiers ride into battle and on the other end of the shot their empty horses emerge without many of the riders they once carried.  In a sea of films about special effects, high concept stories or big names here is a film that is a complete film on its own without any gimmicks, as fun as gimmicks can be (Mi4 for example). This film is an actual experience for those who are open to it. Girl with the Dragon Tattoo was an excellently crafted film that pushed the dark and grittiness, War Horse is a excellently crafted film that pushes the lighter elements but both are equally enjoyable and excellently made. Watching a good Spielberg film is like watching the epitome of what film is. It's like more recently watching Christopher Nolan's films, there are always flaws in any film but films such as theirs are so carefully and purposefully made as films that they are engaging and fully engrossing.  One potential weakness I will admit, though I feel a reviewing of the film would diminish it, is that the film doesn't have as strong an arc as most films. The characters change but so much of it is internal here. The horse's first owner does change but he is absent through the middle of the film, the various other owners also change in varying ways but all during their own vignettes. The horse itself has changed in much the same way as his owner as suggested by the final shot of the film, but perhaps critical audience members won't pick up on this. That said, one could (and I would say should) view this subtlety as purposeful. The film isn't as much about how the boy or the horse has changed but how despite all they've been through they managed to stay so much the same, they maintain what could easily have been lost.  I dunno, I personally love it (5 Stars) but for objectivity I'll give it 4/5 for general audiences, I think most everyone I described at the beginning of this review will like this film if they view it. For those who feel it is heavy handed and melodramatic go and watch Girl with the Dragon Tattoo, its also an excellent film but there is no overt display of emotion to be found there. Thanks for your time

I've read just about every Stephen King book, anthology, magazine novelette and non-fiction he's ever written since Carrie and 11/22/63 achieves two things that he hasn't managed in a long while, in my opinion at least in a decade or more.  #1: Once I started the book I couldn't put it down  #2: The book's conclusion is satisfying. It's believable, it's heartfelt and unforgettable.  Going in I had my doubts whether a writer, even of King's stature, could pull off what seemed to be another in a long line of first-person time traveling experiences. In fact, my expectation was that it would travel from point A to point B and to point C and that it would end as I expected. The difference in 11/22/63, however, is that the path from each of those points isn't linear. As Jake finds out (and I don't think I'm spoiling anything here), like a guitarist playing a melody, pluck or strike one string it affects those strings around it and the tone of the notes that follow. In other words, the past only allows so much improvisation before it begins to collapse on itself. Additionally, I liked Jake, thought Sadie was a well-developed character and thought the back story believably informed the end result.  (Oh, and kudos to Stephen's son novelist Joe Hill to his dad for encouraging changes to the last few pages of the book. Maybe it's why it works so well).  Whether you're a first or long time King fan, you owe it to yourself to pick this one and read it. I promise (or at least hope) you won't be disappointed.   In sum, I have to echo what the USA Today reviewer wrote when this book first came out. This isn't ordinary King. This is extraordinary King.
